Refuting Priority as a Positive Attribute: Two Proofs
Razi provides two complete proofs that priority is not a positive attribute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Razi argues that priority (qabliyyah) cannot be a positive attribute through two logical proofs.

Core Idea
Priority is a relation, not a thing.

Razi continues his refutation of objections to the temporal creation of the world. Here, he tackles the claim that priority is a positive attribute. Through two complete proofs, he demonstrates that priority cannot be an existent quality. The third proof is incomplete, but the logic is clear.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note that Razi presents three proofs, but only the first two are complete. Understand the flow of his argument.
2
Analyze the first proof
The first proof argues that if priority were an attribute, it would be an attribute of non-existence. Since non-existence cannot have existent attributes, priority cannot be existent.
3
Analyze the second proof
The second proof shows that precedence is a relation (idafah) that requires posteriority. If it were an existent attribute, it would require the prior and posterior to exist together, which is contradictory.
4
Reflect on the incomplete third proof
The third proof begins by referencing an earlier argument about yesterday and today, but it is cut off. Consider how the first two proofs already establish the point.
The third proof is not needed to understand the core argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن الشبهة الرابعة إن المفهوم من القبلية لا يعقل أن يكون صفة ثبوتية، ويدل عليه وجوه : أحدها: أنكم إن سلمتم أن كل محدث كان عدمه قبل وجوده فقد جعلتم القبلية صفة للعدم وصفة العدم لا يعقل أن تكون موجودة فالقبلية صفة غير موجودة. وثانيها : أن التقدم إضافة لا تعقل إلا بالإضافة إلى التأخر، والمضافان يوجدان معاً، فلو كان التقدم صفة موجودة لاستحال وجودها إلا مع وجود التأخر، وإذا كان حصول التقدم والتأخر معا لزم أن يكون حصول المتقدم والمتأخر معاً، ولكن المعية تنافي في التقدم والتأخر، فإذن القول بأن القبلية صفة موجودة يفضي إلى هذا المحال فيكون محالا. وثالثها: أنا قد دللنا في أول المسألة على أن الأمس متقدم على اليوم، وليس
And the answer to the fourth doubt: That the concept of priority (al-qabliyyah) cannot be conceived as a positive attribute; and evidences indicate it. One: If you conceded that every originated thing had its non-existence before its existence, then you have made priority an attribute of non-existence, and an attribute of non-existence cannot be conceived as existent, so priority is a non-existent attribute. Two: Precedence (al-taqaddum) is a relation that is only conceived in relation to posteriority (al-ta'akhkhur), and the two relata exist together. If precedence were an existent attribute, its existence would be impossible except with the existence of posteriority. If precedence and posteriority happen together, then the preceding and the posterior must happen together, but simultaneity contradicts precedence and posteriority. So the statement that priority is an existent attribute leads to this impossibility, thus it is impossible. Three: We have demonstrated at the beginning of the discussion that yesterday precedes today, and not
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage is the refutation of the fourth doubt, which claims that priority (such as temporal precedence) is a positive, existent attribute. The author presents three proofs, though the third is incomplete due to page cut-off.
Proof 1: If priority were an attribute, it would be an attribute of non-existence (since before existence, the thing is non-existent). But non-existence cannot have existent attributes, so priority is not existent.
Proof 2: Precedence is a relational concept requiring posteriority. If it were an existent attribute, it would co-exist with posteriority, which would require the prior and posterior to exist simultaneously, contradicting the meaning of priority.
Proof 3 (begun): The author refers to an earlier argument that yesterday precedes today, but the argument is not completed.

Two Views on Existence and Their Implication for the Non-Existent
Razi outlines the implications of two views on existence for the non-existent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the two main views on the relationship between existence and essence lead to different conclusions about whether the non-existent (ma'dum) can be considered a thing (shay').

Core Idea
Whether the non-existent is a thing depends on whether existence is identical to essence or additional.

Razi continues his discussion of existence and essence by examining a crucial implication: if existence is inseparable from essence, then when existence ceases, the essence itself disappears, making it impossible to speak of the non-existent as a thing. But if existence is an additional attribute, the question becomes whether the quiddity can be posited without existence, leading to a major theological disagreement between the Mu'tazila and the Ash'aris.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two views on existence and essence
Recall from the previous lesson that there are two positions: existence identical to essence (philosophers) and existence additional to essence (mutakallimun). Razi now draws implications from each.
This is the foundation for the dispute.
2
See the implication of the first view
If existence is the same as essence, then when existence is gone, the essence is gone too. Therefore, the non-existent cannot be a thing because there is no essence left to be considered a thing.
3
See the implication of the second view
If existence is additional, then it is possible to conceive of the essence without existence. Those who allow this (Mu'tazila) say the non-existent is a thing—a stable quiddity. Those who do not (Ash'aris) say it is not a thing.
This sets up the sectarian divergence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا عرفت هذه المسألة فلنرجع إلى المقصود، فنقول : أما من قال : وجود كل شيء نفس ماهيته لزمه القطع بأنه زال بأنه متى الوجود، فقد زالت الماهية، فالقول بأن المعدوم شيء لا يتصور على مذهب هذا القائل أما من قال : الوجود زائد على الماهية فقد اختلفوا في أنه هل يمكن تقرير الماهية عند زوال صفة الوجود؟ فمن جوز ذلك قال : المعدوم شيء، وعنى به أن الماهية من حيث تكون كتقررة حال ما لا تكون موجودة، ومن لم يجوز ذلك قال : المعدوم ليس بشيء. إذا عرفتم هذا فلنرجع إلى تعيين محل النزاع في هذه المسألة فنقول : المعدوم إما أن يكون واجب العدم ممتنع الوجود، وإما أن يكون جائز الوجود جائز العدم. أما الممتنع فقد اتفقوا على أنه محض وعدم وليس بذات ولا بشيء، وأما المعدوم الذي يجوز عدمه فقد ذهب أصحابنا إلى أنه قبل الوجود نفي محض، وعدم
And when you have understood this issue, let us return to the intended purpose, and say: As for one who says that the existence of each thing is the same as its quiddity, he is necessarily compelled to hold that when existence ceases, the quiddity has ceased; so the statement that the non-existent is a thing is inconceivable according to this view. As for one who says that existence is additional to quiddity, they have differed: Is it possible to posit the quiddity when the attribute of existence ceases? Whoever allows that says: the non-existent is a thing, meaning that the quiddity insofar as it is posited in the state of not being existent. And whoever does not allow that says: the non-existent is not a thing. Now that you have understood this, let us return to specifying the locus of dispute in this issue, and say: The non-existent is either necessarily non-existent, impossible of existence, or possible of existence and possible of non-existence. As for the impossible, they have agreed that it is pure non-existence, not an essence nor a thing. As for the non-existent which is possible of non-existence, our scholars (Ash'aris) hold that before existence it is pure negation and non-existence.
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage is a classification and summary of positions. Razi first reiterates the two main views on existence and essence, then outlines their implications for the status of the non-existent (al-ma'dum). For those who identify existence with essence (like the philosophers), if existence goes, the essence itself ceases, so there is no sense in which the non-existent is a thing. For those who see existence as additional, there is disagreement: some (like the Mu'tazila) hold that the non-existent is a thing (a stable quiddity without existence), while others (like the Ash'aris) hold it is not a thing—it is pure non-existence. Razi then specifies the locus of dispute: the non-existent can be absolutely impossible (like a partner of God) or contingent (like possible created things). All agree the impossible is not a thing. For the contingent non-existent, the Ash'ari position (our scholars) is that before existence it is pure negation and non-existence, not a thing. The text ends mid-sentence, but the point is clear.

Key Terms
شيء (shay')
وجود (wujud)
ماهية (mahiyyah)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The debate about ma'dum is independent of the existence-essence issue.
Correction: Razi shows that the disagreement on ma'dum is directly tied to whether existence is identical to or additional to essence.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, which view necessarily leads to the conclusion that the non-existent is not a thing?
A) Existence identical to essence
B) Existence additional to essence
C) Both views
D) Neither view

Key Takeaway
The status of the non-existent as a thing hinges on whether existence is separate from essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Locus of Dispute: The Ash'ari Position on the Contingent Non-Existent
Razi specifies the locus of dispute and states the Ash'ari view on the contingent non-existent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ash'ari position on the contingent non-existent is defined as pure negation and non-existence, distinguishing it from the Mu'tazili view.

Core Idea
The Ash'aris affirm that the contingent non-existent before existence is pure negation, not a thing.

After laying out the general implications, Razi specifies the exact locus of the dispute. He distinguishes between two kinds of non-existent: the absolutely impossible and the contingent. While all agree the impossible is not a thing, the debate centers on contingent entities that are possible of existence. Razi states the Ash'ari position clearly: before existence, such entities are pure negation and non-existence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories of non-existent
Razi divides the non-existent into two: the necessarily non-existent (impossible, like a partner of God) and the possibly non-existent (contingent, like created things).
Only the second is disputed.
2
Note unanimous agreement on the impossible
All scholars agree that the impossible non-existent is pure non-existence, not an essence or a thing.
This is not the locus of dispute.
3
Learn the Ash'ari view on the contingent non-existent
For contingent things that are not yet existent, the Ash'aris hold that they are pure negation and non-existence before they come into being. This means they are not 'things' in any real sense.
This contrasts with the Mu'tazila who affirm them as stable quiddities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا عرفت هذه المسألة فلنرجع إلى المقصود، فنقول : أما من قال : وجود كل شيء نفس ماهيته لزمه القطع بأنه زال بأنه متى الوجود، فقد زالت الماهية، فالقول بأن المعدوم شيء لا يتصور على مذهب هذا القائل أما من قال : الوجود زائد على الماهية فقد اختلفوا في أنه هل يمكن تقرير الماهية عند زوال صفة الوجود؟ فمن جوز ذلك قال : المعدوم شيء، وعنى به أن الماهية من حيث تكون كتقررة حال ما لا تكون موجودة، ومن لم يجوز ذلك قال : المعدوم ليس بشيء. إذا عرفتم هذا فلنرجع إلى تعيين محل النزاع في هذه المسألة فنقول : المعدوم إما أن يكون واجب العدم ممتنع الوجود، وإما أن يكون جائز الوجود جائز العدم. أما الممتنع فقد اتفقوا على أنه محض وعدم وليس بذات ولا بشيء، وأما المعدوم الذي يجوز عدمه فقد ذهب أصحابنا إلى أنه قبل الوجود نفي محض، وعدم
And when you have understood this issue, let us return to the intended purpose, and say: As for one who says that the existence of each thing is the same as its quiddity, he is necessarily compelled to hold that when existence ceases, the quiddity has ceased; so the statement that the non-existent is a thing is inconceivable according to this view. As for one who says that existence is additional to quiddity, they have differed: Is it possible to posit the quiddity when the attribute of existence ceases? Whoever allows that says: the non-existent is a thing, meaning that the quiddity insofar as it is posited in the state of not being existent. And whoever does not allow that says: the non-existent is not a thing. Now that you have understood this, let us return to specifying the locus of dispute in this issue, and say: The non-existent is either necessarily non-existent, impossible of existence, or possible of existence and possible of non-existence. As for the impossible, they have agreed that it is pure non-existence, not an essence nor a thing. As for the non-existent which is possible of non-existence, our scholars (Ash'aris) hold that before existence it is pure negation and non-existence.
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage is a classification and summary of positions. Razi first reiterates the two main views on existence and essence, then outlines their implications for the status of the non-existent (al-ma'dum). For those who identify existence with essence (like the philosophers), if existence goes, the essence itself ceases, so there is no sense in which the non-existent is a thing. For those who see existence as additional, there is disagreement: some (like the Mu'tazila) hold that the non-existent is a thing (a stable quiddity without existence), while others (like the Ash'aris) hold it is not a thing—it is pure non-existence. Razi then specifies the locus of dispute: the non-existent can be absolutely impossible (like a partner of God) or contingent (like possible created things). All agree the impossible is not a thing. For the contingent non-existent, the Ash'ari position (our scholars) is that before existence it is pure negation and non-existence, not a thing. The text ends mid-sentence, but the point is clear.

Key Terms
معدوم (ma'dum)
نفي محض (nafy mahd)
ممتنع (mumtani')

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Ash'ari position on the contingent non-existent before existence?
A) It is a stable quiddity.
B) It is pure negation and non-existence.
C) It is an essence without attributes.
D) It is a potential existence.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'aris hold that the contingent non-existent before existence is pure non-existence, not a thing.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider that every created thing — including you — was absolutely nothing before God brought it into being, how does that strip away any pride or sense of self-sufficiency you carry today? Will you consciously attribute your very existence to Him at every breath?
?
You often worry about future events that do not yet exist — your plans, your health, your reputation. According to this lesson, these contingent things are pure negation before God creates them. Does your anxiety reflect a false belief that those future possibilities have some independent reality? How can you surrender them to the One who alone brings things from nothing to being?
?
If the Ash'aris are right that the non-existent before creation is pure non-existence, then the only reality in your life is what God has actually brought into existence right now. What would change in your heart if you truly accepted that your past mistakes and your future hopes have no substance apart from God's creative will? Are you ready to live fully in the present moment of His creation?

The Dispute Over the Reality of Non-Existents
Razi introduces the dispute over whether the non-existent is a thing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two opposing Mu'tazilite views on the ontological status of non-existent things and the central question of the second issue.

Core Idea
The non-existent's reality is debated: is it a thing or nothing at all?

Imam al-Razi begins the second issue by laying out the two rival positions within the Mu'tazila regarding whether non-existent entities (al-ma'dūm) possess any reality, quiddity, or essence. This dispute is not about existence per se but about the nature of things before they come into being. The passage sets the stage for Razi's own arguments against the view that non-existents are things.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the literal translation. Notice the two opposing views presented.
The phrase 'ف،' at the beginning is a textual remnant; focus on the content after it.
2
Identify key terms
Identify the terms shay' (thing), dhāt (essence), māhiyya (quiddity), and haqīqa (reality). Understand how they relate to the positions.
These terms are central to kalam discussions of ontology.
3
Reflect on the dispute's significance
Ask yourself: why does it matter whether the non-existent is a thing? Consider implications for divine knowledge and creation.
Razi will later argue against the view that non-existents are things.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة الثانية: ف، وليس بشيء ولا بذات، وهذا قول أبي الحسين البصري من المعتزلة، وذهب أكثر شيوخ المعتزلة إلى أنها ماهيات وذوات وحقائق حالتي وجودها وعدمها، فهذا هو تلخيص محل النزاع.
The Second Issue: For, it is not a thing nor an essence, and this is the statement of Abu al-Husayn al-Basri of the Mu'tazila, and the majority of the leading Mu'tazila went to the view that they (i.e., non-existents) are quiddities (māhiyāt), essences (dhawāt), and realities (ḥaqā'iq) in both states of their existence and non-existence. So this is the summary of the point of dispute.
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage defines the scope of the second issue in Razi's work. It presents two opposing Mu'tazilite positions on the ontological status of non-existent things (al-ma'dūm). The first view, attributed to Abu al-Husayn al-Basri, denies that the non-existent is a thing (shay') or an essence (dhāt). The second view, held by the majority of Mu'tazilite scholars, asserts that non-existents are quiddities (māhiyāt), essences (dhawāt), and realities (ḥaqā'iq) regardless of whether they exist or not. Razi then states that this is the summary of the point of contention, setting up his forthcoming proofs against the view that the non-existent is a thing.

Key Terms

ذات (dhāt: essence)
حقيقة (ḥaqīqa: reality)
معدوم (ma'dūm: non-existent)
شيء (shay': thing)

Correction: Razi is merely summarizing the Mu'tazilite dispute; his own argument comes later.

The second issue in Razi's work concerns whether non-existent things possess quiddity, essence, and reality; the Mu'tazilites were divided on this question.

First Proof: The Impossibility of Non-Existent Quiddities Being Real
Razi presents the first proof against non-existents being things.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's first proof that non-existent quiddities cannot be considered real things in external reality.

Core Idea
Razi argues that if quiddities were real in non-existence, they would be both existent and non-existent.

In this passage, Imam al-Razi presents his first proof against the claim that non‑existent quiddities are ‘things’ in the external world. He shows that identifying the ‘establishment’ (taqarrur) of a quiddity with something other than existence leads to an impossible contradiction. The proof relies on the distinction between conception and assent, and on the principle that what is shared among quiddities (their being established) must differ from what distinguishes them. Let us follow his argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and its translation
Begin by reading the Arabic text from the source and its literal English translation. Notice the key terms: māhiyya, taḥaqquq, taqarrur, wujūd, ʿadam, taṣawwur, taṣdīq.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the main conditional argument
Razi starts with a conditional: if quiddities were realized externally without existence, then (a) they would have something common (realization) beyond their particulars, and (b) that realization is identical to existence, so they would be existent while non-existent, which is impossible.
This is the core logical move.
3
Examine the second part about conception and assent
Razi supports his proof by noting that we perceive a difference between forming a concept of blackness and assenting that it is established in non-existence. If establishment were not additional, the two would collapse.
This is a self-evident distinction.
4
Conclude the impossibility
Since the assumption leads to a contradiction, the claim that quiddities are established in non-existence is impossible.
This refutes the realist view of non-existents.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البرهان الأول : أن هذه الماهيات لو كانت متحققة في الخارج حال عرائها عن الوجود لكانت في كونها متحققة خارج الذهن ومباينة لخصوصياتها المعينة، وما به المشاركة غير ما به الممايزة فيلزم أن يكون تحقق كل ماهية تقررها زائدا على خصوصيتها، ولا معنى للوجود إلا هذا التحقق والتقرر فيلزم كونها موجودة حال كونها معدومة، وذلك محال. ومما يدل على أن كونها مختلفة خارج الذهن أمر زائد على ماهيتها المخصوصة: أنا إذا قلنا : السواد متقرر حال العدم فنحن ندرك تفرقة بديهية بين ذلك التصور وهذا التصديق، ولولا أن تقررها خارج الذهن حال عدمها أمر زائد على ماهيتها وإلا لما بقي فرق بين التصور والتصديق وذلك محال على ما قررناه فثبت أن الماهيات لو كانت متقررة حال عدمها، لكانت موجودة حال عدمها ولما كان محالا علمنا أن القول بأن الماهيات متقررة حال عدمها محال.
The first proof: That these quiddities (māhiyāt), if they were realized (mutḥaqqiqa) in external reality (al-khārij) in the state of being devoid of existence (al-wujūd), would be, in their being realized outside the mind and distinct from their particular characteristics, such that what is shared is different from what distinguishes, so it would entail that the realization (taḥaqquq) of every quiddity, its being established (taqarrur), is additional to its particularity, and there is no meaning of existence except this realization and establishment, so it would entail that they are existent while being non-existent, and that is impossible. And among the things that indicate that their being different outside the mind is something additional to their specific quiddity is that if we say: 'Blackness is established (mutaqarrir) in the state of non-existence,' we perceive a self-evident difference between that conception (taṣawwur) and this assent (taṣdīq). And were it not that its establishment outside the mind in the state of non-existence is something additional to its quiddity, there would be no difference between conception and assent, and that is impossible according to what we have established. So it is proven that if quiddities were established in the state of non-existence, they would be existent while non-existent, and since that is impossible, we know that the statement that quiddities are established in the state of non-existence is impossible.
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage contains Razi's first proof that the non-existent is not a thing. The argument proceeds as follows: If quiddities (māhiyāt) were realized in external reality even when stripped of existence, they would have a reality outside the mind that is distinct from their particular characteristics. The common factor (being realized) would differ from what distinguishes them. This would mean that the realization of each quiddity, its being established (taqarrur), is something additional to its particularity. But the meaning of existence is precisely this realization and establishment. Therefore, quiddities would be existent while being non-existent, which is a contradiction. Razi then supports this with a second point: If we say 'Blackness is established in non-existence,' we can distinguish between forming a concept of blackness (taṣawwur) and assenting that it is established (taṣdīq). This difference would vanish if establishment were not additional to the quiddity. Since we do perceive a difference, the establishment must be something extra, i.e., existence. Hence, quiddities cannot be established in non-existence without being existent. This concludes the proof that the claim is impossible.

Key Terms
ماهية (māhiyya: quiddity)
تحقق (taḥaqquq: realization)
تقرر (taqarrur: establishment)
وجود (wujūd: existence)
عدم (ʿadam: non-existence)
تصور (taṣawwur: conception)
تصديق (taṣdīq: assent)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think Razi is simply redefining 'existence' as 'realization' without proof.
Correction: Razi argues that the term 'existence' is used for the same reality as 'realization' (taḥaqquq/taqarrur) in external reality. His proof shows that if you grant that quiddities have a form of realization outside the mind, you are granting them existence, which leads to a contradiction.

Checkpoint
In the first proof, what does Razi say would happen if quiddities were established (mutaqarrir) in non-existence?
A) They would be more perfect than existing things.
B) They would be existent while non-existent, which is impossible.
C) They would be indistinguishable from one another.
D) They would be purely mental concepts.

Key Takeaway
Razi's first proof demonstrates that non-existent quiddities cannot be considered real things because attributing any external realization to them forces them to be existent, which is contradictory.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If every moment of your existence is a direct granting from God—since non-existence has no reality from which it could emerge—how does this change your awareness of His constant, sustaining presence in your life? Will you pause today to thank Him not just for what you have, but that you are?
?
Razi shows a self-evident difference between conceiving a truth and truly assenting to it. Is there a truth about your relationship with your Creator that you have merely conceived but never deeply assented to—such as His absolute power or your complete dependence? What step can you take today to move from conception to heartfelt, living assent?
?
The proof forces you to see that nothing can come from non-existence; everything that exists is given existence by another. Have you been living as if your own existence and abilities are self-sufficient? How would your daily actions change if you genuinely believed that each thought, each breath, is a direct gift from the One who brings things from nothing?

The Finitude of Non-Existent Essences
Razi disproves the infinitude of non-existent essences

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's second proof that non-existent essences cannot be infinite, and why this refutes their subsistence.

Core Idea
The set of non-existent essences cannot be infinite because it can be decreased.

Razi presents a logical refutation of the claim that non-existent essences have reality (subsist in non-existence). The argument focuses on whether the set of these essences is finite or infinite, showing both alternatives lead to absurdity. Let's dive into his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the logical structure: Razi states the two alternatives, rejects finitude by agreement, and then disproves infinitude through a quantitative argument.
Pay attention to the phrase 'otherwise a thing with another would be like it without another' – this is the key logical step.
2
Identify the two alternatives
Razi says the essences must be either finite or infinite. He rejects finitude by agreement with the opponent. Then he shows infinitude leads to contradiction.
The 'agreement' might be that the opponent already denies finitude, so Razi focuses on infinitude.
3
Follow the reductio argument
Razi assumes infinitude and then argues that after some essences become existent, the remaining set is smaller than the original. Since a smaller set cannot be infinite, the remaining set is finite. Then both parts (those that came out and those remaining) are finite, so the sum is finite – contradicting infinitude.
The crucial premise is that if the whole set is infinite, removing some should not make it smaller – but it does, so the set must be finite.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Since both finitude (by agreement) and infinitude (by logical argument) are false, the original assumption that essences subsist in non-existence is false.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البرهان الثاني : أن الذوات الثابتة في العدم إما أن تكون متناهية أو غير متناهية والقسمان باطلان، فبطل القول بثبوت الذوات المعدومة، أما أن كونها متناهية باطل فبالاتفاق، وأما أن كونها غير متناهية باطل فذلك لأن مجموع الذوات المعدومة حين ما لم يخرج منها شيء إلى الوجود كانت أكثر مما يبقى منها في العدم بعد خروج بعضها إلى الوجود وإلا لكان الشيء مع غيره كهو لا مع غيره، وذلك محال، وإذا كان كذلك كانت الذوات التي بقيت الآن في العدم أقل من مجموع الذوات التي كانت معدومة عند ما لم يخرج شيء منها إلى الوجود، وما كان أقل من غيره فهو متناه، فالذوات التي بقيت الآن في العدم متناهية، والتي خرج منها إلى الوجود متناهية ومجموع المتناهي مع المتناهي يكون متناهياً، فثبت أن القول بكون الذوات غير متناهية محال.
The second proof: That the essences (al-dhawāt) subsisting (al-thābita) in non-existence are either finite (mutanāhiya) or infinite (ghayr mutanāhiya), and both divisions are false, so the statement of the subsistence of non-existent essences is invalid. As for their being finite being false, it is by agreement. As for their being infinite being false, that is because the totality of non-existent essences when none of them had come into existence was greater than what remains in non-existence after some of them have come into existence; otherwise a thing with another would be like it without another, and that is impossible. And if that is so, then the essences that now remain in non-existence are less than the totality of essences that were non-existent when none had come into existence, and whatever is less than another is finite. So the essences that now remain in non-existence are finite, and those that have come into existence are finite, and the sum of the finite with the finite is finite. So it is proven that the statement that the essences are infinite is impossible.
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
In this second proof, Razi uses a reductio ad absurdum argument regarding the quantitative status of non-existent essences (dhawāt). He assumes that if non-existent essences subsist (thābita) in non-existence, they must be either finite or infinite. He asserts that finitude is false by agreement (presumably because the opponent holds they are infinite), so he focuses on disproving infinitude. The argument: Before any essences came into existence, the totality of non-existent essences was greater than the set that remains after some have become existent. This is because if they were equal, then the whole set with some removed would be equal to itself, which is impossible. Thus, the remaining set is smaller, and any set smaller than another is finite. Since both the ones that have come out and the ones that remain are finite, their total is also finite. Therefore, infinite is impossible. Hence, the only alternative (finitude) is also false by agreement, so the initial assumption that essences subsist in non-existence is false. This is a logical refutation of the reality of non-existent essences.

Key Terms
ذات (dhāt)
ثبوت (thubūt)
متناهي (mutanāhī)
غير متناهي (ghayr mutanāhī)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The set of non-existent essences can be infinite in the same way that natural numbers are infinite, and removing some still leaves an infinite set.
Correction: Razi argues that if essences are real, their set must be finite because a smaller subset indicates finitude. The argument relies on the premise that an infinite set cannot become smaller by removal – which was a common metaphysical assumption in his context. Modern mathematics, however, shows that infinite sets can have proper subsets of the same size. Thus, the argument is valid only within the framework of the author's metaphysical principles.

Checkpoint
Why does Razi reject the possibility that non-existent essences are infinite?
A) Because an infinite set cannot be decreased.
B) Because the set that remains after some have become existent is smaller, and a smaller set cannot be infinite.
C) Because the total of two infinites would be infinite.
D) Because finitude is true by agreement.

Key Takeaway
Razi's second proof shows that the subsistence of non-existent essences is false because both their finitude and infinitude lead to contradictions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Before you were born, you were among those non-existent essences. God chose to bring you into existence from that finite set. Does knowing that your existence was a specific, limited possibility change how you view the gift of being alive today? How will you respond to that gift?
?
Razi argues that the set of non-existent essences is finite because it can be decreased. Your life is one of those decreases. What does it mean for you that you are no longer just a possibility but an actual reality? What responsibility does that place on you?
?
If your existence were part of an infinite set, it might feel less significant. But Razi shows that all essences are finite, meaning God's creation is purposeful and limited. Does that make you feel more precious or more accountable? How will that shape your actions today?

The Third Proof: From Possibility to Origination
Razi presents a syllogistic proof for the createdness of quiddities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's third proof that quiddities are possible in themselves, which entails their createdness and refutes the notion that non-existents are things.

Core Idea
Quiddities are possible, so they must be originated, not eternal.

Razi presents a concise syllogism to prove that quiddities (the essences of things) are created and preceded by non-existence. This argument uses a premise already established: everything possible is originated. By showing that quiddities are possible, he concludes they are created, thereby refuting those who claim that non-existents are real things.

Guided Path
1
Read the Passage Carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, noting the key terms. Then read the translation to understand the flow of the argument.
Pay attention to terms like 'mumkin', 'muḥdath', and 'wājib al-wujūd'.
2
Identify the Syllogism
Break down the argument into its premises and conclusion. Notice how Razi uses the principle that 'every possible is originated' as a major premise.
The syllogism: All quiddities are possible. All possible things are originated. Therefore, quiddities are originated.
3
Examine the Justification of the Minor Premise
Why does Razi say quiddities are possible? Trace his reasoning: if they were necessary, there would be multiple Necessary Existents, which is impossible.
This is a reductio ad absurdum argument against the necessity of quiddities.
4
Reflect on the Refutation
How does this proof refute the view that non-existents are things? Connect the conclusion that quiddities are preceded by non-existence to the broader theological context.
If quiddities are created, they were non-existent before creation, so non-existence is not a thing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البرهان الثالث : هذه الماهيات من حيث إنها هي ممكنة لذواتها، وكل ممكن محدث فهذه الماهيات من حيث هي هي محدثة فيلزم أن تكون هذه الماهيات مسبوقة بالنفي المحض والعدم الصرف، وهو المطلوب. وإنما قلنا بأن هذه الماهيات ممكنة لذواتها وذلك لأنها لو كانت واجبة التقرر لذواتها في الخارج لكانت واجبة التقرر في الخارج ممتنعة الزوال، ولا معنى لواجب الوجود إلا ذلك، وحينئذ يكون واجب الوجود أكثر من واحد، وذلك محال، وإذا ثبت أنها في كونها متقررة في الأعيان ممكنة وثبت أن كل ممكن محدث، وثبت أنها من حيث إنها ماهيات محدثة، وذلك يبطل القول بأن المعدوم شيء، وتمام تقرر هذا البرهان قد تقدم في مسألة حدوث الأجسام.
The third proof: These quiddities (māhiyāt), inasmuch as they are possible (mumkina) in themselves, and every possible is originated (muḥdath), so these quiddities insofar as they are quiddities are originated, thus it follows that these quiddities are preceded by pure negation and sheer non-existence, and that is what is sought. And we only say that these quiddities are possible in themselves because if they were necessary of establishment (wājibat al-taqarrur) in themselves in external reality, they would be necessarily established in external reality, impossible of removal, and there is no meaning of 'Necessary of Existence' (wājib al-wujūd) except that. Then the Necessary of Existence would be more than one, and that is impossible. And when it is established that they are possible in their being established in external entities, and it is established that every possible is originated, and it is established that they are originated insofar as they are quiddities, that invalidates the statement that the non-existent is a thing. The complete elaboration of this proof has preceded in the issue of the origination (ḥudūth) of bodies.
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage presents Razi's third proof. It begins with a syllogism: Quiddities (māhiyāt) are possible (mumkina) in themselves. Every possible is originated (muḥdath). Therefore, quiddities are originated, which means they are preceded by pure non-existence, refuting the claim that non-existents are things. Razi then justifies the first premise: why are quiddities possible? If they were necessarily established in themselves externally, they would be necessarily existent and impossible to remove, which is precisely the meaning of the Necessary of Existence (wājib al-wujūd). This would entail multiple necessary existents, which is impossible. Therefore, quiddities are possible, not necessary. Since they are possible, they are originated, as already argued in the discussion on the origination of bodies. Thus, the non-existent cannot be a thing. Razi refers to a previous proof for the principle that every possible is originated.

Key Terms
ممكن (mumkin: possible)
واجب الوجود (wājib al-wujūd: Necessary of Existence)
محدث (muḥdath: originated)
تقرر (taqarrur: establishment)
نفي محض (nafy maḥḍ: pure negation)
عدم صرف (ʿadam ṣirf: sheer non-existence)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think this proof is circular because it assumes quiddities are non-existents to prove they are created.
Correction: Razi does not assume non-existence; he argues that because quiddities are possible (not necessary), they must be created. The conclusion that they are preceded by non-existence follows from their createdness, not vice versa.

Checkpoint
What would quiddities be if they were necessarily established in themselves, according to Razi?
A) They would be eternal.
B) They would be multiple necessary existents.
C) They would be impossible.
D) They would be non-existent.

Key Takeaway
Razi's third proof demonstrates that quiddities, being possible, must be originated, which supports the Islamic doctrine of creation ex nihilo.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If quiddities are possible in themselves and not necessary, what does that imply about your own existence? Are you rooted in necessity or contingency? How does that affect your reliance on the Necessary of Existence?
?
The proof invalidates the notion that non-existents are things. Do you ever treat your own potentialities as if they were already actual? How does this shape your view of your past and future?
?
Razi uses a reductio to avoid multiple necessary existents. In your life, do you ever elevate created things to the status of necessity, making them indispensable? How can this proof help you rethink what you cling to?

The Quranic Proof from Divine Power
Razi presents a Quranic proof that quiddities are non-existents in pre-eternity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi uses the Quranic verse 'Allah is over all things powerful' to prove that quiddities are pure non-existence in pre-eternity, establishing that only God is truly existent.

Core Idea
God's power over 'all things' proves quiddities are nothing until He creates them.

Razi now turns to a scriptural proof, grounding his argument in the Quran. The verse 'Allah is over all things powerful' (Al-Baqarah 2:284) becomes the foundation for a logical chain that shows non-existent quiddities are not 'things' in themselves. Let's follow how he unpacks this verse to reach that conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the verse and claim
Start by reading the verse and Razi's initial claim that the name 'thing' includes quiddities. Notice how he expands the scope of the verse.
2
Trace the logical steps
Follow the reasoning: God is powerful over quiddities → His power must effect something → effecter precedes effect → quiddities are non-existent before creation.
Pay attention to the terms 'establishing' and 'annihilating'.
3
Understand the conclusion
See how Razi concludes that quiddities in pre-eternity are 'pure negation and sheer non-existence'. This is the key point against the idea that non-existents are 'things'.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this matters: if quiddities are not eternal, then only God exists necessarily, and all creation depends on His power.
This proof supports the contingency of the world.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البرهان الرابع : قوله تعالى: ﴿ وَاللهُ عَلَى كُلِّ شَيْءٍ قَدِيرُ ﴾ [البَقَرَة : ٢٨٤] وجه الاستدلال بالآية أن اسم الشيء يتناول الماهيات، فوجب أن يكون الله تعالى قادرًا على تلك الماهيات، وإنما يكون قادرًا عليها لو كان لقدرته صلاحية أن تؤثر في تلك الماهيات تقريرًا وإبطالاً، ومتى كان الأمر كذلك كان وجود الله تعالى متقدمًا على تقرير تلك الماهيات، لوجوب تقدم المؤثر على الأثر، ومتى كان الأمر كذلك ثبت أن الماهيات بأسرها نفي محض، وعدم صرف في الأزل، وذلك هو المطلوب.
The fourth proof: The saying of the Most High: {And Allah is over all things powerful} [Al-Baqarah 2:284]. The aspect of inference from the verse is that the name 'thing' (al-shay') encompasses quiddities (māhiyāt), so it is necessary that Allah Most High is powerful over those quiddities. And He would only be powerful over them if His power (qudra) had the capacity to effect (tu'aththir) in those quiddities, establishing (taqrīran) or annihilating (ibṭālan). And whenever this is the case, the existence of Allah Most High is prior to the establishment (taqarrur) of those quiddities, due to the necessity of the priority of the effecter (al-mu'aththir) over the effect (al-athar). And whenever this is the case, it is established that the quiddities in their entirety are pure negation and sheer non-existence in pre-eternity (al-azal), and that is what is sought.
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
Razi's fourth proof is scriptural. He cites the Quranic verse 'Allah is over all things powerful' (Al-Baqarah 2:284). The argument: The term 'thing' (shay') in the verse includes quiddities (māhiyāt). Therefore, God is powerful over those quiddities. For God to be truly powerful over them, His power must be able to effect something in them, either by establishing them (bringing them into existence) or annihilating them (removing them from existence). This implies that God's existence is prior to the establishment of these quiddities, because the effecter must precede the effect. Hence, the quiddities in pre-eternity were nothing but pure negation and sheer non-existence. This proves that the non-existent is not a thing, because they are not 'things' in pre-eternity but become things only through God's power.

Key Terms
قادر (qādir: powerful)
شيء (shay': thing)
قدرة (qudra: power)
تأثير (ta'thīr: effecting)
تقرير (taqrīr: establishment)
إبطال (ibṭāl: annihilation)
مؤثر (mu'aththir: effecter)
أثر (athar: effect)
أزل (azal: pre-eternity)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the verse only refers to existing things, so God's power does not apply to non-existent quiddities.
Correction: Razi argues that 'all things' includes quiddities (potential beings), and God's power over them means they are effects of His power, hence non-existent before His act.

Checkpoint
According to the fourth proof, what does God's power over quiddities entail about their pre-eternal status?
A) They were already existing.
B) They were pure negation and non-existence.
C) They were only mental.
D) They were eternal.

Key Takeaway
Razi's fourth proof uses the Quranic verse about God's power to demonstrate that quiddities are non-existent in pre-eternity, affirming that only God exists necessarily.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If every aspect of your existence—your health, wealth, relationships, and even your very self—was absolutely nothing in pre-eternity and exists only because God continuously wills it, why do you still act as if these things are permanent or self-sufficient? What one change can you make today to live with the reality that you are nothing without His constant creation?
?
The verse 'Allah is over all things powerful' means His power truly effects everything, including your own thoughts and actions. In what area of your life are you tempted to think that God's power does not reach—perhaps a struggle you think you must solve alone? How will you hand that over to Him today?
?
If quiddities are pure non-existence before God's act, then your very identity is contingent on His will. How does this truth comfort or challenge you when you face failure or loss? What specific prayer can you say to remind yourself that you exist only by His power?

Opponents' First Argument: Distinction Among Non-Existents
Razi presents the opponents' first argument with three sub-proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the opponents' first argument that non-existents are distinct and therefore have determinate realities, and you will be able to identify and explain each of the three sub-proofs they offer.

Core Idea
Non-existents are distinct in themselves, so they must have realities.

In this passage, Imam al-Razi presents the first argument from those who claim the non-existent is a 'thing'. They argue that non-existents are distinct from one another, and such distinction requires that each has a determinate reality. To prove this distinction, they offer three examples from everyday experience. This lesson walks through each sub-proof and its underlying logic.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then read the English translation. Pay attention to the structure: the main claim followed by three numbered sub-proofs.
The passage uses technical terms like 'māhiyya' and 'ḥaqīqa' – note them.
2
Identify the core claim
Find the sentence that states the main argument: non-existents are distinct, so they have realities. Notice how the opponents define 'thing' as having a determinate reality.
The claim is in the first few lines: 'أن المعدومات متميزة في أنفسها...'
3
Examine each sub-proof
Read each of the three sub-proofs (الأول, الثاني, الثالث). For each, identify the example used (sunrise, movement, desire) and how it demonstrates distinction among non-existents.
The third sub-proof is longer and involves desires and dislikes.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
After studying the sub-proofs, consider how they jointly support the claim that non-existents have distinct realities. Ask yourself: could these distinctions be merely mental? The passage suggests they are real distinctions.
This argument is a key point in the debate over the reality of non-existents.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحتج القائلون بأن المعدوم شيء بأمرين : الحجة الأولى : أن المعدومات متميزة في أنفسها وكل ما يتميز بعضه عن البعض فلا بد وأن تكون لأنفسها حقائق متعينة ولا معنى لقولنا المعدوم شيء إلا ذلك، والذي يدل على أن المعدومات متميزة بعضها عن البعض حال عدمها وجوه الأول : وهو أنا نعلم أن إذا تطلع الشمس من المشرق ولا تطلع من المغرب، وهذان المطلوعان معدومان في الحال، ونحن الآن نعلم امتياز كل واحد منهما عن الآخر، وهذا يدل على وقوع الامتياز في المعدومات. الثاني : هو أنا قادرون على الحركة يمنة ويسرة ولسنا قادرين على الطيران إلى السماء فقد تميز أحد المعدومين عن الثاني من حيث إن أحدهما مقدور لنا والآخر غير مقدور لنا، وهذا الامتياز واقع حال العدم، فثبت وقوع الامتياز في المعدومات. الثالث : هو أنما نجد من أنفسنا أنا نريد أن تحدث لنا أموال وأولاد وخيرات وسعادات وأن لا تحدث لنا أنواع الأمراض والآفات مع كون كل واحد من هذين القسمين معدومًا فلولا تميز أحد المعدومين عن الآخر حال كونه معدوما بماهيته المعينة وحقيقته المخصوصة وإلا لامتنع كون أحدهما مراد الوقوع والآخر مكروه الوقوع.
And those who say that the non-existent is a thing adduced two arguments: The first argument: That non-existents (al-ma'dūmāt) are distinct (mutamayyiza) in themselves, and everything some of which are distinct from others must have determinate realities (ḥaqā'iq mu'ayyana) for themselves, and there is no meaning to our saying 'the non-existent is a thing' except that. And what indicates that non-existents are distinct from one another in the state of non-existence are aspects: The first: That we know that the sun rises from the east and does not rise from the west, and these two risings are currently non-existent, and we now know the distinction of each one from the other, and this indicates the occurrence of distinction among non-existents. The second: That we are able to move right and left and we are not able to fly to the sky, so one of the two non-existents is distinct from the second in that one is within our power (maqdūr) and the other is not within our power, and this distinction occurs in the state of non-existence, so the occurrence of distinction among non-existents is established. The third: That we find in ourselves that we desire that there occur for us wealth, children, goods, and happiness, and that there not occur for us types of diseases and afflictions, although each of these two categories is non-existent. Were it not for the distinction of one non-existent from the other in the state of its being non-existent by its specific quiddity (māhiyyatuhu al-mu'ayyana) and its particular reality (ḥaqīqatuhu al-makhṣūṣa), then it would be impossible for one to be desired to occur and the other to be disliked to occur.
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage presents the first argument (al-ḥujja al-ūlā) of the opponents who claim the non-existent is a thing. The core claim is that non-existents are distinct (mutamayyiza) from each other in themselves, and such distinction requires that they have determinate realities (ḥaqā'iq mu'ayyana). That is exactly what it means to call them 'things'. To prove that non-existents are distinct, the opponents offer three sub-proofs (the fourth is cut off). First sub-proof: We know that a future sunrise from the east is different from one from the west, even though both are currently non-existent. This distinction is known, so non-existents must have distinct realities. Second sub-proof: We have the power to move right or left but not to fly to the sky. These two potential actions are non-existent yet one is within our power and the other is not, showing distinction among non-existents. Third sub-proof: We hope for wealth and children and fear diseases. These are non-existent, yet we desire one and dislike the other, which would be impossible unless they have distinct essences. Thus, non-existents have specific quiddities (māhiyāt) and realities (ḥaqā'iq).

Key Terms
متميز (mutamayyiz: distinct)
حقيقة (ḥaqīqa: reality)
ماهية (māhiyya: quiddity)
معدوم (ma'dūm: non-existent)
مقدور (maqdūr: within power)
مراد (murād: desired)
مكروه (makrūh: disliked)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think these arguments merely show we can conceive of non-existents as distinct, but the opponents claim real distinction in themselves, not just mental.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that non-existents are distinct 'in themselves' (في أنفسها) and 'in the state of non-existence' (حال عدمها), implying real, not merely conceptual, distinction.

Checkpoint
What is the common premise in all three sub-proofs of the opponents' first argument?
A) Non-existents are known only through revelation.
B) Non-existents are distinct from each other while being non-existent.
C) Non-existents are identical to existents.
D) Non-existents are impossible to distinguish.

Key Takeaway
The opponents argue that non-existents are distinct in themselves, requiring that each has a determinate reality, and they support this with three examples from everyday experience.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You hope for wealth and children and fear diseases—things that are not yet real. But if these non-existents have no distinct reality in themselves, why do your hopes and fears feel so real? Are you treating them as already real in your heart, and what does that say about your trust in Allah's decree?
?
Think of a decision you made today based on a future possibility—like choosing a path of action because you believed one outcome was possible and another was not. Did you act as though those possibilities had distinct realities? How does awareness of this argument change your view of how you weigh options and rely on Allah's knowledge?
?
The opponents argue that our ability to distinguish non-existents shows they have real essences. Consider a fear you have about the future—like a fear of illness. Does that fear make the illness somehow 'present' in your mind? Does this lesson challenge you to rethink whether your fears are based on real distinctions or mere illusions?

The Fourth Proof: Essences Are Not Temporally Originated
Razi presents the fourth proof that essences cannot be temporally originated.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's fourth proof that essences (mahiyat) are not temporally originated and that the non-existent (ma'dum) has fixed determination.

Core Idea
If essences were created, they could be destroyed — but that is impossible, so essences are self-subsistent.

Razi now presents a sophisticated logical argument to show that the essences of things — like 'substance' and 'blackness' — cannot be originated by an external cause. The proof hinges on a careful analysis of what it would mean for an essence to cease to be itself. By examining the logical impossibility of such a change, Razi establishes that essences are fixed and self-subsistent, supporting the claim that the non-existent is a determinate thing.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the complete Arabic passage once, noticing the formal style. Then read the translation to understand the argument's flow.
The passage begins with 'The Second Issue' and then presents the fourth proof.
2
Identify the core premise
Find the key claim: if essences were originated, they'd need a creator; but that leads to an impossibility. Understand the entailment (mulazama).
The entailment is that anything originated requires a constituent (mukawwin) and realizer (muhaqqiq).
3
Analyze the contradiction argument
Focus on the part where Razi shows that if essences were dependent on a cause, then removing the cause would destroy the essence, which is impossible because it would require a contradiction (e.g., blackness not being blackness).
Pay attention to the logical analysis of the proposition about blackness.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Understand that the proof demonstrates that essences cannot be temporally originated, implying they are self-subsistent — thus the non-existent has a fixed reality as a thing.
This supports the broader issue that the non-existent (ma'dum) is a thing with determinate nature.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة الثانية
الحجة الرابعة : الماهيات لو كانت متجددة لكان تجددها بإحداث محدث وإيقاع قادر وهذا محال فذلك مثله بيان الملازمة أن كل ما حدث بعد أن لم يكن فلا بد له من مكون ومحقق وبيان أنه يمتنع أن يكون تحقق تلك الماهيات وتقررها بإحداث محدث، وذلك لأن كل ما كان تحققه بسبب غيره لزم من فرض عد ذلك الغير عدم ذلك الأثر ، فلو كان كون الجوهر جوهرًا وكون السواد سوادًا لأجل الأثر، فلو كان كون الجوهر جوهرًا وكون السواد سوادًا لأجل سبب منفصل لزم عند فرض عدم ذلك السبب المنفصل أن يخرج الجوهر عن كونه جوهرا والسواد عن كونه سواداً، وذلك محال.
لأن قولنا : 'السواد خرج عن كونه سوادا وبطل كونه سوادًا' قضية موضوعها السواد ومحمولها خرج عن كونه سوادًا أو بطل كونه سوادًا، ومن شرط صحة القضية إمكان اجتماع موضوعها ومحمولها معاً، ومعلوم أن اجتماع النقيضين محال، فثبت أنه يستحيل قولنا : السواد خرج عن كونه سوادًا وبطل كونه سواداً، وإذا كان الأمر كذلك ثبت أنه يمتنع إسناد تقرر الماهية وتكونها إلى إيجاد موجد، وإيقاع فاعل.
The Second Issue. The Fourth Proof: If essences (mahiyat) were temporally originated (mutajaddida), their origination would be by the bringing into existence of a bringer into existence (muhdith) and the effectuation of a powerful agent (qadir), and this is impossible, so the same applies to it. The explanation of the entailment: everything that comes to be after not having been must have a constituent (mukawwin) and a realizer (muhaqqiq). And the explanation that it is impossible for the actualization (tahaqquq) and determination (taqarrur) of those essences to be by the bringing into existence of a bringer into existence is because everything whose actualization is due to something else entails, given the assumption of the non-existence of that other, the non-existence of that effect. So if the being-a-substance (kawn al-jawhar jawharan) and the being-blackness (kawn al-sawad sawadan) were due to an effect, and if the being-a-substance and being-blackness were due to a separate cause, then given the assumption of the non-existence of that separate cause, it would necessarily follow that the substance would cease to be a substance and blackness would cease to be blackness, and that is impossible. Because our statement: 'Blackness ceased to be blackness and its being blackness became null' is a proposition whose subject is blackness and whose predicate is 'ceased to be blackness' or 'its being blackness became null', and one of the conditions for the validity of a proposition is the possibility of the simultaneous existence of its subject and predicate. It is known that the conjunction of two contradictories (naqidayn) is impossible. So it is established that our statement 'Blackness ceased to be blackness and its being blackness became null' is impossible. And if the matter is so, it is established that it is impossible to attribute the determination (taqarrur) and becoming (takawwun) of the essence to the bringing into existence of a bringer into existence (ijad mūjid) or the effectuation of an agent (iqa' fa'il).
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth proof that the non-existent (ma'dum) is a determinate thing. It begins by stating the second issue (al-mas'ala al-thaniya), then introduces the fourth proof. The argument proceeds as follows: If essences (mahiyat) were temporally originated (mutajaddida), their origination would require a creator (muhdith) and a powerful agent (qadir). But that is impossible, so the supposed origination is impossible. The proof establishes an entailment (mulazama): anything that comes to be after non-existence must have a constituent (mukawwin) and a realizer (muhaqqiq). Then it argues that it is impossible for the actualization of essences to be by a creator, because if something's actualization is due to an external cause, then assuming the non-existence of that cause would entail the non-existence of the effect. So if the essential properties (like being a substance or being blackness) were due to an external cause, then if that cause were absent, the substance would cease to be a substance and blackness would cease to be blackness — which is impossible because it would require a contradiction: blackness not being blackness. The proof uses logical analysis of the proposition 'Blackness ceased to be blackness' — noting that it is impossible for the subject (blackness) and predicate (not being blackness) to coexist simultaneously, which violates the condition for a meaningful proposition. Therefore, it is impossible for the determination of essences to be dependent on an external cause, implying that essences are self-subsistent and thus non-existent things have fixed reality.

Key Terms
mahiyat (essences)
mutajaddid (temporally originated)
muhdith (bringer into existence)
qadir (powerful agent)
mukawwin (constituent)
muhaqqiq (realizer)
taqarrur (determination)
naqidayn (two contradictories)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The fourth proof denies any role for a creator regarding essences.
Correction: The proof does not deny that existence in the external world may be caused; it argues that the essences themselves (e.g., 'substance') are not temporally originated and are self-subsistent.

Checkpoint
What is the main conclusion of the fourth proof?
A) Essences are temporally originated and require a creator.
B) Essences cannot be attributed to an external cause because that would entail impossible contradictions.
C) The non-existent (ma'dum) is absolutely nothing.
D) Blackness is not a real quality.

Key Takeaway
Essences are self-subsistent and not temporally originated; thus the non-existent has fixed determination.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the essence of blackness cannot cease to be blackness, then there are truths about reality that are not dependent on any act of creation. Are you willing to accept that there are fixed truths that even God's power does not alter? How does this challenge any subconscious belief that everything is relative or subject to change?
?
This proof shows that the very nature of things (their 'whatness') is self-subsistent. Consider a quality you deeply value, like mercy or justice. If its essence is eternally fixed, does that give you a sense of security in a changing world? Or does it unsettle you because it implies there are immutable standards above your own preferences?
?
Razi argues that if essences were created, they could be destroyed. In your life, what 'essences' do you treat as if they are fragile—like your identity or worth? Are you living as if these could be lost, contrary to the logical permanence this proof establishes? What would change if you truly believed that your core nature is uncreated and indestructible?

Impossible Things Are Not Realities
Razi asserts that impossible things are not essences, realities, or quiddities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's initial claim that impossible things (mumtani'at) are not essences, realities, or quiddities, and why this sets up his refutation of the opponent's view.

Core Idea
Impossible things are mind-dependent concepts, not real entities.

Razi begins his refutation by stating a key premise: the impossible objects that we conceive in our minds are not genuine essences or realities. This may seem obvious, but it directly challenges the opponent's assumption that whatever is known must have some kind of existence. In this lesson, we will examine this opening statement and see how it frames the entire argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the single sentence in Arabic. Notice the three terms: ذوات, حقائق, ماهيات.
2
Understand the terms
These terms all refer to real, extra-mental being. Razi is denying that the impossible things have any of these.
3
Reflect on the argumentative role
Why does Razi lead with this? He is setting the conclusion that mental awareness does not grant reality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أن هذه الممتنعات ليست ذوات ولا حقائق ولا ماهيات.
الصورة الثانية: العلم بالمركبات، كما إذا تصورنا بحرًا من زئبق، وجبلاً من وزبرجد، فإن البحر والجبل عبارة عن جواهر موصوفة بالتركيب والتأليف، وموصوفة بألوان مختلفة وأشكال مخصوصة وعندكم الثابت في العدم إنما هو ذوات الجواهر والأعراض، فإما أن يكون الجوهر موصوفًا بالعرض فذلك غير ثابت في العدم البتة والجبل إنما يكون جبلاً لكون الجواهر موصوفة بالأعراض والتركيبات، فإذن المشعور الذهني حاصل في هذه الصورة مع أن الشعور به غير حاصل البتة، لا في العدم ولا في الوجود.
الصورة الثالثة: العلم بالنسب والإضافات مثل علمنا بحصول هذا الجسم في هذا الحيز دون ذلك الحيز وطلوع الشمس غدًا من المشرق لا من المغرب، ولا معنى لطلوع الشمس من المشرق إلا حصول ذاتها في ذلك الحيز دون هذا الحيز، ولا شك ولا نزاع أن هذه النسب غير ثابتة في العدم لأن كون الشمس المعدوم حاصلة في المكان المعدوم محال في بداهة العقل وأنتم ساعدتم أيضًا على أنه لا حصول ولا تقرر لأمثال هذه النسب في العدم.
الصورة الرابعة: إنما نعلم امتياز العدم عن الوجود وامتياز النفي المحض والسلب الصرف عن الثبوت ومسمى النفي والصرف والسلب المحض محكوم عليه بكونه متميزًا عن الثبوت مع أنه يستحيل أن يكون لمسمى النفي المحض ثبوت؛ لأن المراد من النفي المحض ما هو مقابل لمسمى الثبوت ومنافٍ له، فلو كان له ثبوت بوجه ما لكان منافي الشيء ومناقضه نفس ذلك الشيء وهو محال، ولأن الخصم يساعد على أن مسمى العدم غير ثابت في العدم.
الصورة الخامسة: إنا نحكم على الماهية المعدومة بأنها غير موجودة، فقبل اتصاف تلك الماهية بالوجود نحن نتصور ماهية الوجود ونميز في أذهاننا بين مسمى الوجود وبين مسمى الجوهر والسواد ولذلك فإن الخصم يقول: مسمى الجوهر والسواد ثابت في العدم ومسمى الجوهر غير ثابت في العدم، فثبت أنا نتصور ماهية الوجود وحقيقته قبل صيرورة الماهية موصوفة بالوجود، فلو لزم من مجرد هذا التمييز الذهني والشعور العقلي كون الشعور به حاصلاً في العدم لزم كون ماهية الوجود مقررة في العدم وذلك محال لأن العدم نقيض الوجود، والنقيضان لا يجتمعان، ولأن الخصم أيضًا يساعد على أن ماهية الوجود غير متقررة في العدم.
الصورة السادسة: نحن نعقل ماهية المؤثرية وماهية المتأثرية، ونميز بين كل واحد منهما وبين الآخر، وبينهما غيرهما، مع أن الخصم يساعد على أن المعدوم لا يكون مؤثرًا ولا متأثرًا، ولا يتقرر حال العدم بمسمى المؤثرية ومسمى المتأثرية، فالشعور الذهني والإدراك العقلي حاصل مع أن المشعور به غير حاصل، وكذا كون الشيء فوق غيره وتحت غيره ويمينًا وشمالاً، ويدخل فيها جميع النسب والإضافات.
That these impossible things are not essences, nor realities, nor quiddities.
The second image: knowledge of composites, such as when we imagine a sea of mercury and a mountain of emerald. For the sea and the mountain are expressions for substances described by composition and arrangement, and described by various colors and specific shapes. According to you, that which is established in non-existence is only the essences of substances and accidents. As for the substance being described by an accident, that is not established in non-existence at all. The mountain is only a mountain because the substances are described by accidents and compositions. Thus, mental awareness is obtained in this image, while the awareness of it is not obtained at all, neither in non-existence nor in existence.
The third image: knowledge of relations and attributes, such as our knowledge of this body being in this place rather than that place, and the sun rising tomorrow from the east not from the west. There is no meaning to the sun rising from the east except the occurrence of its essence in that place rather than this place. There is no doubt or dispute that these relations are not established in non-existence, because the non-existent sun occurring in the non-existent place is impossible in the clarity of reason, and you also agree that there is no occurrence or establishment for such relations in non-existence.
The fourth image: we know the distinction of non-existence from existence and the distinction of pure negation and absolute privation from affirmation. The term 'negation' and 'privation' and 'absolute privation' is judged to be distinct from affirmation, while it is impossible for the term 'pure negation' to have affirmation, because the meaning of pure negation is that which is opposite to the term 'affirmation' and contradictory to it. So if it had affirmation in any respect, then the contradictory of a thing would be the very same thing, which is impossible. And because the opponent agrees that the term 'non-existence' is not established in non-existence.
The fifth image: we judge that the non-existent quiddity is non-existent. Before that quiddity is described by existence, we conceive the quiddity of existence and distinguish in our minds between the term 'existence' and the term 'substance' and 'blackness'. Therefore the opponent says: the term 'substance' and 'blackness' is established in non-existence, and the term 'existence' is not established in non-existence. So it is established that we conceive the quiddity of existence and its reality before the quiddity becomes described by existence. So if from mere mental distinction and intellectual awareness it necessarily follows that the awareness of it occurs in non-existence, then it would necessarily follow that the quiddity of existence is established in non-existence, and that is impossible because non-existence is the opposite of existence, and opposites cannot coexist. And because the opponent also agrees that the quiddity of existence is not established in non-existence.
The sixth image: we conceive the quiddity of effecting and the quiddity of being affected, and we distinguish between each one of them and the other, and between them and others, while the opponent agrees that the non-existent is neither an effecter nor affected, and the term 'effecting' and 'being affected' are not established in the state of non-existence. Thus mental awareness and intellectual perception are obtained while the perceived object is not obtained. Similarly, the thing being above another and below another and right and left, and all relations and attributes enter into that.
Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This passage is a refutation by demonstration through counterexamples. Razi presents six distinct images (suwar) to argue that mental conception and awareness can occur without the object having any real existence in the external world. The overall purpose is to undermine the opponent's claim that the non-existent (ma'dum) must be a thing because it can be known. 

First, Razi asserts that these impossible objects (mumtani'at) are not essences, realities, or quiddities. Then he proceeds to the images:

- Second image: We can imagine composites like a mercury sea or emerald mountain. According to the opponent, only the essences of substances and accidents exist in non-existence, but the accidents and compositions are not there. Yet we have a mental picture of the whole thing.
- Third image: Relations and attributes such as 'being in a place' or 'rising from the east' are clearly understood, but these relations cannot exist in non-existence because that would require the non-existent sun to be in a non-existent place, which is absurd. The opponent agrees such relations are not established in non-existence.
- Fourth image: We distinguish between non-existence and existence, and between pure negation and affirmation. Pure negation cannot have any sort of existence because it is opposite to affirmation, yet we understand it as distinct.
- Fifth image: We conceive the quiddity of existence itself before something exists. If mental awareness implied real existence, then the quiddity of existence would have to be established in non-existence, but that is contradictory because existence and non-existence are opposites.
- Sixth image: We conceive 'effecting' and 'being affected' and distinguish them, yet the non-existent cannot be an agent or patient. The same applies to spatial relations like above/below.

Thus, in every case, mental awareness exists without the object being real. The key is that the opponent must either deny these obvious mental phenomena or admit that being known does not entail being a thing.

Key Terms
ممتنعات
ذوات
حقائق
ماهيات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think Razi is denying that the non-existent can be known at all.
Correction: Razi is only denying that the non-existent has real essences; he agrees it can be known mentally.

Checkpoint
What does Razi claim about the mumtani'at?
A) They have essences in non-existence.
B) They are not essences, realities, or quiddities.
C) They are imaginary and unknowable.
D) They are the same as existents.

Key Takeaway
Impossible things conceived in the mind have no extra-mental reality; they are not essences or quiddities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
What impossible desires or fears do you cling to, even though they have no real essence? How can you use Razi's assertion to release their hold on your heart and align your concerns with what is truly real?
?
If impossible things have no reality, what does that imply about your anxiety over events that can never happen? Will you today choose to treat such thoughts as mere concepts rather than realities that control you?
?
Are there beliefs about yourself—like 'I can never change' or 'This sin is too big for me'—that you treat as real essences? How does this lesson challenge you to see them as false constructs, and what step will you take to dismantle them?

Composites: Sea of Mercury and Emerald Mountain
Razi presents a counterexample of composite objects to show mental awareness without external existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi uses the example of a mercury sea and emerald mountain to show that mental awareness of composites does not require those composites to exist externally.

Core Idea
Composite objects can be imagined without their parts existing in reality.

Razi's second image tackles the case of composite objects that we clearly conceive, like a sea of mercury or a mountain of emerald. According to the opponent's own admission, the accidents and compositions that make something a 'sea' or 'mountain' are not established in non-existence. Yet we can form a complete mental image. How is this possible if mental awareness implies real existence? This lesson examines Razi's argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the second image carefully. Note the example and the opponent's stance.
2
Identify the opponent's view
The opponent holds that only essences of substances and accidents exist in non-existence, not their combinations.
This is key to Razi's argument.
3
Understand the conclusion
Razi concludes that mental awareness is present without the composite object existing either in non-existence or existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Think of a worry that has been consuming you—a fear about something that hasn't happened. If that worry is a 'mountain of emerald' constructed in your mind, does it have any real existence outside of your thoughts? How can you remind yourself of this truth when anxiety strikes?
?

Is it possible that you have constructed an image of Allah based on your own imagination rather than on what He has revealed? How can you ensure your worship is directed to the real God, not a mental composite?

Relations: Body in Place and Sun Rising
Razi presents a counterexample of relations to show mental awareness without external reality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi uses relations (like 'being in a place' or 'rising from the east') to argue that mental awareness of relations does not require those relations to exist in non-existence.

Core Idea
Relations are mentally conceived but have no extra-mental reality in non-existence.

Razi's third image turns to relations and attributes, such as a body being in a specific place or the sun rising from the east. These are clearly understood, but they involve relations that cannot exist in non-existence because that would require a non-existent subject in a non-existent location, which is absurd. Even the opponent admits such relations are not established in non-existence. Yet we have knowledge of them. This lesson explores this argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the third image. Note how Razi frames the opponent's agreement.
2
Focus on the examples
Consider the body in a place and the sun's rising direction. These involve relations that require existent relata.
3
Grasp the logical impossibility
A non-existent sun in a non-existent place is impossible. Yet we still conceive the relation.

Source Text
